# Chapter 1

## **Chapter 1**

Maya’s confession began with a thermostat. Sixty-eight degrees for him, a masculine comfort. Seventy-six for me, a feminine compromise. The numbers glowed in the dark like a scoreboard for a war we never declared, fought over kilowatts and passive aggression instead of love.



It wasn’t on purpose. It was a Tuesday. The air in the city tasted of wet pavement and distant, regretful smoke, the kind that drifted over from the controlled burns in the northern forests—a preventative measure that always felt like closing the barn door after the horse had not only bolted but drowned. I was late for my shift at the Green Canopy, a café that sold eight-dollar oat milk lattes and a fiction of sustainability. I took the stairs to the third-floor walk-up two at a time, my lungs burning in the thin, over-processed atmosphere, and shoved the key into the lock of 3B.

The door swung open to a wall of sound. Not music. A roar. The visceral, white-noise scream of a dozen industrial fans. The apartment was a cathedral of moving air. Every window was sealed shut with secondary glazing and silicone, but between them and the exterior walls stood a battalion of fans, their blades a silver blur, pulling the outside through HEPA filters the size of suitcase. The air in here wasn’t just clean; it was *aggressive*. It had intention. It stole the breath from your mouth before you could properly claim it.

And there, in the eye of this manufactured storm, was Leo. He stood at the kitchen counter, which was not a kitchen counter but a laboratory bench. He was calibrating a handheld sensor with the focus of a bomb disposal expert, his brow furrowed, his mouth a flat line of concentration. He wore noise-cancelling headphones over his dark, close-cropped curls. He didn’t hear me come in.

This was our shared climate confessional. His: a fortress of pure data, a war against particulate matter. Mine: a silent, screaming guilt I carried in the hollow of my chest, a guilt about everything from the carbon footprint of my imported coffee beans to the sheer, indulgent existence of my own breath.

“Leo!” I shouted over the fan-whine.

He didn’t flinch. I walked into the stream of one of the fans, letting it whip my hair across my face, and waved a hand in his periphery. His eyes flicked up. They were the colour of old pennies, greenish-brown, always slightly narrowed as if assessing the chemical composition of whatever they saw. He lifted one ear of the headphones.

“You’re creating a vortex,” I said. “Mrs. Gable downstairs says her teacups are rattling.”

“Atmospheric pressure differential,” he said, his voice low and matter-of-fact. “Temporary. I’m testing the filter load from the burn-off. PM2.5 levels are thirty times the WHO standard outside. In here, they’re negligible.” He offered me the sensor. A tiny digital screen glowed with numbers: 4. *Four*. A saintly number.

I looked at it, then at him. “It’s a Tuesday morning. People are trying to have… Tuesdays.”

“The data doesn’t care what day it is, Mara.” He went back to his calibration. “You’re breathing carcinogens with every ‘Tuesday’ breath out there. Here, you’re not.”

That was Leo. Not “we’re safe.” *You’re safe*. He built this clean-air ark for himself, and I, by the accident of being his roommate and the one who could actually cover the rent, was a stowaway. A stowaway who left the porthole open sometimes because she missed the smell of rain, even poisoned rain.

I dropped my bag on the reclaimed-wood chair—a piece he’d analyzed for formaldehyde off-gassing—and went to the window. Through the double-paned glass, the city was a grey and green smudge. The vertical gardens on the opposite building dripped from the morning fog-system, their lushness a desperate, beautiful lie. Everything here was a performance of survival. We were all actors in a play where the apocalypse was a slow, administrative process.

“I need to talk to you,” I said, not turning around.

“I’m listening.” The screech of a fan changed pitch as he unplugged it.

“It’s about the trip.”

The silence that followed as the last fan whirred down was deafening. It was a thick, expensive silence, purchased by carbon-filters and obsessive energy consumption.

“What trip?” he asked, though he knew.

“The one to Cypress Hills. In two weeks. For Alana’s wedding. You said you’d think about coming.”

I heard the soft click of the sensor being set down. “I did think. The answer is no.”

I turned. He was looking at me, his arms crossed. He was wearing a grey henley, frayed at the elbows, and his posture was a closed system. “Leo, it’s my sister. It’s in a park. It’s supposed to be ‘low-impact.’ Biodegradable confetti, locally-sourced kale for the dinner, the whole pastoral fantasy.”

“A park 300 kilometers away,” he said, pulling a tablet towards him. A few taps, and a map bloomed with angry red lines. “Accessed by a fossil-fuel vehicle, or a diesel-powered coach. The ‘local’ kale will have been transported by refrigerated truck. The wedding party is what, fifty people? Sixty? Their combined travel footprint alone is obscene. The confetti, even if made from fallen leaves, is an unnecessary disturbance to a local ecosystem. A wedding.” He said the word like it was a type of oil spill.

My guilt, that familiar beast, stirred. He was right. Of course he was right. Every celebration was a crime scene now; every joy had a shadow-cost. But another feeling, hotter and sharper, rose beneath it. “So we just stop? We stop going to see the people we love? We stop marking things? We just sit in our filtered boxes and wait to be efficiently recycled?”

“I mark things,” he said quietly, gesturing to the tablet. “I track the decline. I note the ppm of CO2 on significant dates. It’s a different kind of observance.”

“It’s a funeral,” I snapped. “You’re conducting a perpetual funeral.”

“Someone has to be the witness, Mara. Someone has to not look away.”

The air between us, so meticulously cleaned, felt charged with the unsaid. This was our dance. My messy, emotional culpability versus his sterile, precise despair. We were two sides of the same sinking coin. I leaned against the window, the glass cool on my forehead. “I have to go. She’s my sister.”

“I know.”

“I want you to come with me.”

“Why?”

The question hung there. Because I’m afraid to go into that much green space alone? Because your grim certainty is a perverse comfort next to everyone else’s forced cheer? Because when I’m with you, my own guilt feels less like a personal failing and more like a shared, factual condition?

“Because,” I said, finally, “you keep me honest.”

He was silent for a long moment. He picked up the sensor again, looked at its pristine reading, then out the window at the toxic haze. “Honest,” he repeated, as if tasting the word. “Alright.”

I blinked. “Alright?”

“I’ll go. On one condition.”

“Anything.”

“We don’t drive. We take the electric rail to the nearest station. It’s forty kilometers from the park. We bike the rest.”

“*Bike*? With our suits and dresses?”

“Panniers exist. It’s a condition.”

I imagined us, me in my moss-green bridesmaid’s dress, him in his one good jacket, pedalling through prairie backroads like some kind of apocalyptic comedy troupe. I imagined Alana’s perfectly contoured face when we rolled up, sweaty and dusty. A laugh bubbled up in my chest, a strange, light feeling amidst the heaviness. “Deal.”

“And we run a carbon budget. For the entire trip. Everything. Travel, food, even the energy cost of the music amplification.”
“A carbon budget. For a wedding.”
“For existing,” he corrected. “Yes.”

I pushed off from the window and walked over to his bench. I took the sensor from his hand. Our fingers brushed. His were always slightly cool. I looked at the number: 4. I took a deep, deliberate breath of the costliest, cleanest air in the city. I was stealing it, every molecule. I was complicit in his fortress, a beneficiary of his war.

“Start the spreadsheet,” I said.

He almost smiled. It was just a twitch at the corner of his mouth, but I saw it. “It’s already started.”

***

The Green Canopy was quiet that afternoon. I moved behind the counter in a haze, steaming oat milk and grinding beans that had sailed across an ocean. My confession for the day: I loved the smell. I loved the ritual. I loved the tiny, stolen pleasure of a perfect microfoam rose I could pour on a latte. Leo’s world was one of subtraction—remove the particulates, eliminate the miles, reduce the impact. Mine was one of fraught addition—adding little beauties onto the scale of sin, hoping somehow they’d balance, knowing they never could.

Sasha came in at three. Sasha, who worked in corporate ESG—Environmental, Social, and Governance—a field Leo referred to as “conscience laundering.” She was all sharp blazers and sharper smiles, a master of making doom palatable.

“Mara, my saviour,” she sighed, leaning on the counter. “A long black. And tell me something good about the world.”

“The philodendron in the corner put out a new leaf,” I said, pulling a cup.

“See? Resilience. I’ll take it.” She watched me work. “You’re pensive. Fight with your climate husband?”

“He’s not my husband.” The machine hissed. “We’re going to my sister’s wedding together. He’s making us bike the last forty clicks.”

Sasha threw her head back and laughed, a rich, unburdened sound. “Oh, I love him. I absolutely love him. He’s so… pure. A fanatic in a henley. You should record the trip. It’s a metaphor waiting to happen.”

“It’s not a metaphor, Sasha. It’s just a pain in the ass.”

“Everything’s a metaphor now, darling. The personal is planetary, didn’t you get the memo?” She accepted the coffee, took a sip. “Seriously, though. How do you live with him? Doesn’t the constant audit of existence just… exhaust you?”

I wiped down the steam wand. I thought of the roar of the fans, the sanctity of the number 4 on the sensor. “It’s honest,” I said, repeating my own word from the morning. “It’s exhausting, but it’s honest. Everything else feels like a story we’re telling ourselves so we can sleep at night.”

Sasha’s smile softened, turned professional. “Stories are important, Mara. They’re what allow people to move through the day without jumping off a bridge. My whole job is finding the right story.” She tapped her nails on the recycled cardboard sleeve. “Bring him to the thing on Friday. The rooftop thing. It’ll be good for him. Maybe he’ll see not all of us are monsters.”

“What thing?”
“Oh, just a little mixer. Investors, thinkers, doers. Sustainable fintech, green crypto, that whole scene. Very low-key. Very carbon-offset.” She winked. “Tell him the canapés will be locally-sourced insect protein. That’ll get him.”

After she left, I stood in the quiet café. The philodendron’s new leaf was a heart-shaped, waxy green, unfurling towards the UV grow light we used to supplement the grey sky. A story. Maybe that’s what I was doing with Leo. Building a story where our twin anxieties—his clinical, mine poetic—could coexist. A story where we could bike to a wedding and call it a solution.

I took out my phone and texted him. *Sasha invited us to a rooftop mixer on Friday. Green tech people. I think you should hate it.*

The reply came almost instantly. *I already do. What’s the address?*

I smiled. That was the other thread in our story. Our shared, secret relish for judging the efforts of others. It was our intimacy.

At closing time, as I was locking the door, my phone buzzed again. It was a photo from Leo. Not of a spreadsheet. It was a picture of two touring bikes leaning against a graffiti-covered wall. They looked old, sturdy, relics from a simpler time. The message read: *Found them. Rental. They have panniers. The audit begins.*

I stared at the image. The bikes looked both ridiculous and noble. I thought of the open road, the wind that wouldn’t be filtered, the smell of real fields, of dirt and growing things. I thought of Leo, his body moving in rhythm beside mine, not in a sealed box but out in the wounded, beautiful world. For a second, I didn’t feel the guilt. I felt a thrill.

It was quickly followed by the familiar, sinking weight. Because wanting this—wanting the journey, the wedding, the experience—was the heart of the problem. Desire was the original carbon sin.

I typed back: *They look perfect.*

It wasn’t a lie. They looked like perfect instruments of our shared confession.

# Chapter 2

## **Chapter 2**

The rooftop “mixer” was a masterclass in cognitive dissonance.

It was held atop the Vertica Building, a spire of glass and steel praised for its rainwater recycling system and bird-safe glazing. The elevator ride alone used enough energy to power Leo’s filtration system for a week, a fact he murmured to me as the floors ticked by, his body tense beside mine. He’d worn his jacket, the same one he’d wear to bike forty kilometers, and looked like a botanist who’d wandered into a casino.

The doors opened onto a curated breeze. Massive, slow-rotating fans moved the thick evening air. The space was a jungle of potted tropical plants—ferns, palms, monstera with leaves like stretched leather—each a thirsty exile from a hotter, wetter world, kept alive by a subcutaneous network of drip lines. The people were a similar species: beautiful, transplanted, surviving on artifice. They clutched glasses of sparkling water infused with “regenerative” cucumber and held bamboo plates of food so meticulously sourced you could practically trace the farmer’s genealogy.

“PM2.5 is probably low,” Leo said, his eyes scanning the crowd not for people but for ventilation ducts. “But the VOC off-gassing from all this plastic polymer flooring is a cocktail. And the embodied energy in these plants…”

“Just breathe, Leo,” Sasha materialized beside us, a vision in emerald green silk that probably had its own sustainability certificate. “It’s metaphorical breathing. Look, Mara, you came! And you brought the conscience of the party.” She air-kissed me and extended a hand to Leo, which he shook with the caution of someone checking for toxins. “Leo. So glad you could surface. Drink?”

“I have water,” he said, lifting a reusable canteen from his pocket.

“Of course you do.” Sasha’s smile didn’t falter. “Mingle, you two. The man by the vertical herb wall is from a lab growing coral in a vat. The woman near the solar-powered fairy lights invented a blockchain for carbon credits. It’s a garden of earthly delights, if your delight is techno-solutions.”

She swept away. Leo and I stood like stones in a stream as the crowd flowed around us.

“Coral in a vat,” he said, deadpan. “Because the ocean is now just a inconvenient location.”

“It’s hope, Leo,” I said, though it sounded weak even to me.

“It’s hospice,” he corrected. He took a small, notebook-sized sensor from his inner pocket—a different one, sleeker—and pointed it discreetly at the crowd. “The CO2 concentration from all these people exhaling is already 200 ppm above baseline. This party is literally creating its own micro-climate.”

I took his arm, feeling the tight muscle beneath the wool. “Stop auditing for one hour. Please. For me. Try to see it as… human.”

He looked at me, his penny-coloured eyes reflecting the fairy lights. “This isn’t human, Mara. This is post-human. This is the wake.” But he slipped the sensor back into his pocket.

We drifted. I listened to snippets of conversation: “leveraging impact investing,” “circular economy protocols,” “gamifying consumer carbon choices.” It was a language of salvation through systems, of buying and selling our way out of the pit. My guilt here took a different flavour. It was less sharp than with Leo, more diffuse. Here, I felt stupid. I didn’t speak the language of redemption. I just served the coffee that fueled it.

Then I saw him. A man standing slightly apart, near the edge of the roof, looking not at the people but at the cityscape beyond the glass barrier. He was tall, with a sort of relaxed stillness. He held a drink but wasn’t drinking it. He wore a simple, well-cut shirt, sleeves rolled up. And he was watching a bee. A single, confused bumblebee, bumping against a potted lavender plant, then veering towards the towering lights, disoriented.

I felt Leo follow my gaze. “*Bombus terrestris*,” he said softly. “Probably from a rooftop hive a few blocks over. The light pollution has destroyed its navigation. It’ll exhaust itself and die before dawn.”

Before I could stop myself, I was walking towards the man, towards the bee. I didn’t know why. Maybe just to do one small, non-digital, non-financialized thing.

As I approached, the man turned. He had a kind, weathered face, early forties maybe, with smile lines that seemed earned, not practiced. He saw me looking at the bee.

“Lost,” he said. His voice was quiet, gravelly.

“Yeah,” I said. I reached up slowly, cupped my hands near the bee as it hovered, dazed. I didn’t try to catch it, just provided a darker, quieter place for it to orient to. After a moment, it landed on the back of my hand, its tiny feet tickling. It rested there, furry abdomen pulsing.

“You’re good with creatures in distress,” the man said.

“Just bees.” I kept very still. Out of the corner of my eye, I saw Leo watching us, his expression unreadable.

“I’m Silas,” the man said.

“Mara.”

“You’re not like the others here, Mara.”

“I serve them coffee.”

He smiled, and it reached his eyes. “Ah. The most essential service.” He nodded towards the bee. “You know, they say if bees disappear, we have four years. That’s always stuck with me. Not centuries, not decades. Four years. The timeline is so brutally short.”

The bee lifted from my hand, circled my head once, and then, miraculously, flew upwards and away, over the glass barrier, into the smoggy twilight. Maybe to its hive. Maybe to die. But it was gone.

“There’s a certain clarity in a short timeline,” I said, surprising myself.

“Is there?” Silas asked, genuinely curious.

“No room for bullshit. No time for mixers.”

He laughed, a real, warm sound that cut through the ambient techno-murmur. “I like that.” He sipped his drink. “I’m here because my friend invited me. He’s trying to get me to invest in a cloud-seeding drone startup. I’m a farmer. Or, I was. Now I mostly just manage the decline.”

A farmer. The word felt ancient, solid, in this floating garden. “Where?”

“South. Near what’s left of the Cypress Hills, actually.”

The coincidence was a small electric shock. “My sister’s getting married there. In two weeks.”

“Is she?” His gaze grew more focused. “At the Interprovincial Park? The ‘rewilding’ wedding package?”

“You know it?”

“I helped source the ‘local’ kale,” he said, a wry twist to his mouth. “And I advised on the ‘low-impact’ site. It’s… well, it’s something.”

Leo had joined us, silent as a shadow. I felt his presence at my shoulder.

“This is Leo,” I said. “My roommate. Leo, this is Silas. He’s a farmer near the wedding.”

Leo gave a curt nod. “You provided the kale.”

Silas’s eyes flickered with amusement. “I did. And the bitter knowledge that it took more diesel to get my small harvest to the park’s preferred caterer than it would have to truck in ten times the amount from the hydroponic vertical farm in the city. But the story is better. The carbon ledger is… creative.”

Leo’s posture changed almost imperceptibly. It was the slightest relaxation. He’d found someone who spoke a dialect of his language. “Creative accounting is the primary technology of the Anthropocene,” Leo said.

“The only growth industry,” Silas agreed. He looked between us. “So you’re going to the wedding? How are you getting there?”

“Train,” I said. “Then bike.”

Silas’s eyebrows rose. He looked at Leo, then back at me. “You’re biking? From Swift Current?”

“You know the route,” Leo said, not a question.

“I’ve driven it. It’s beautiful. It’s also long. And the winds… they’re not what they used to be. More erratic. Stronger.” He studied us. “You have a place to stay the night before? The park doesn’t allow arrivals after dark to ‘preserve nocturnal ecology.’ Another line item in the story.”

We hadn’t thought that far. I’d assumed we’d just arrive. Leo’s spreadsheet probably had a cell for it, blank and accusatory.

Silas saw the answer on our faces. He pulled a card from his pocket—real paper, thick, with a faint texture of chaff. “That’s my place. It’s on your way. Twenty clicks from the park entrance, down a old grid road. It’s not a B&B. It’s a farmhouse. It has a roof, beds, and food that doesn’t have a story. It just *is*. If you get caught, or if you want to break the journey… the offer’s there.” He handed the card to me, not to Leo.

I took it. *Silas Vale. Vale Family Landholdings.* A phone number. No email. No website.

“Thank you,” I said. “We might… we’ll see.”

“Do,” he said. His eyes held mine for a beat too long, a look of understanding, of shared exhaustion with the narratives. Then he nodded to Leo. “Good luck with the audit.” And he melted back into the crowd, towards the man with the cloud-seeding drones.

Leo was looking at the card in my hand. “A farmer,” he said.

“He seemed real.”

“Real is not a synonym for sustainable.”

“I know.” I tucked the card into my clutch. “But he knew about the winds.”

We stayed another twenty minutes. Leo spent most of it talking to a desperate-looking woman from a carbon-capture concrete company, asking her detailed questions about the energy inputs of the calcination process until she excused herself. I watched Silas from across the roof. He listened, he nodded, he smiled his weary smile, but he didn’t pull out a tablet. He just watched the people, like he was observing a strange and fragile crop.

On the elevator down, the silence was different. The filtered roar of the apartment would be a relief after the low hum of the rooftop.

“He was right about the timeline,” Leo said abruptly, staring at the descending numbers. “Four years for the bees. For us, it’s longer, but the curve is the same. Exponential. We’re at the part of the curve where it feels like it’s bending, but it’s just about to go vertical.” He glanced at me. “A wedding in that context is…”

“Absurd,” I finished.

“Yes.”

“And biking there is…”

“Also absurd.”

“But it’s our absurdity,” I said.

He didn’t answer. When the doors opened in the lobby, he said, “We should factor in the high probability of headwinds. It will increase our caloric expenditure. We’ll need to pack more food. The weight will increase drag.”

“I’ll pack dense calories,” I said, following him out into the humid, unfiltered night. “Nuts. Chocolate.”

“Dark chocolate has a lower dairy footprint,” he said, automatically. Then he stopped on the sidewalk. The city lights reflected in his eyes. “He was looking at you.”

The statement was a data point, delivered flat.

“Who? Silas?”

“Yes.”

“He was being friendly. He’s a friendly guy who grows kale for tragic weddings.”

Leo started walking again, his hands in his pockets. “He’s a landowner. ‘Landholdings.’ That implies capital, heritage. His carbon footprint, even as a ‘farmer,’ is likely multiples of ours. His offer of shelter is a gesture of that privilege.”

I hurried to catch up. “Or it’s just kindness, Leo. A bed for two cyclists. Not everything is a ledger.”

“Everything is,” he said, but his heart wasn’t in it. He was tired. The party, the talk, the sheer weight of the performance had drained him. It drained me too, but in a different way. It left me restless.

Back in the apartment, the fans were off. The silence was a physical presence. Leo went straight to his bench and opened his laptop, the glow lighting his face. Adding data from the evening, no doubt. The rooftop’s carbon signature. The food miles of the canapés. The cost of my lost bee.

I stood in the middle of the room, still in my dress, holding Silas’s card. I thought of his weathered hands, his quiet laugh, his word: *decline*. He didn’t say “fight” or “solution.” He said *manage the decline*. It was a confession, stark and unadorned. It felt truer than anything I’d heard all night.

I went to the window and looked out. Somewhere out there in the vast, wounded dark, past the train lines and the shrinking fields, was a farmhouse on a grid road. A place with no story. I had a sudden, fierce craving for it. For the silence that wasn’t purchased, for the darkness unbroken by fairy lights, for the taste of food that just *was*.

It was a new kind of guilt. Not for what I was taking, but for what I wanted.

Behind me, I heard the soft tap of Leo’s fingers on the keys. Writing the story of our night in numbers. I slipped Silas’s card into a drawer. I didn’t mention it again. But I knew, and I think Leo knew, that the spreadsheet for the trip had just acquired a new, unquantifiable variable.

# Chapter 3

## **Chapter 3**

The train car smelled of recycled air and anticipation. It was full of weekenders, families with backpacks, couples leaning into each other. We were the odd ones, surrounded by panniers stuffed not with hiking gear but with garment bags and shoe boxes, our touring bikes locked in a special carriage up ahead. Leo had spent the morning doing a final equipment check, weighing each pannier on a kitchen scale, adjusting the distribution to the gram. My guilt had manifested as over-packing: I’d brought three different outfits for a two-day trip, a jar of homemade granola, a book of poetry printed on hemp paper, and a nervous energy that vibrated in my teeth.

Leo sat by the window, his sensor on the small table, tracking the rising CO2 levels from forty-seven exhaling passengers. He’d already calculated the per-person carbon mile of the electric train journey and was now, I suspected, mentally critiquing the sandwich packaging of the man across the aisle.

“It’s starting to look like land,” I said, pressing my forehead to the glass. The endless suburban sprawl had given way to patches of brown and green, fields in various states of early summer struggle. The sky was a vast, pale bowl, streaked with lingering haze.

“Monoculture,” Leo said, not looking up from his tablet. “Wheat, canola. Soils depleted. The green zones you see are just the ones that got rain at the right time. It’s a patchwork of luck, not health.”

“Just look at it, Leo. Please. For five minutes, just see it without diagnosing it.”

He sighed, a short, sharp exhale, but he lifted his gaze. The landscape scrolled past, a blur of fence lines and lonely clumps of trees sheltering farmsteads. His face, in profile against the light, was solemn. “I am seeing it. I see the irrigation circles that are no longer full. I see the windbreaks that are dying. I see the colour of the soil—it’s too pale. It’s tired.”

“It’s still here,” I whispered.

“For now.”

We lapsed into silence. I opened my book but couldn’t focus. The poems were about loss, about rivers. I closed it. My phone buzzed. A text from Alana.

*>> You on the train? Did you remember the shoes? Mom is having a meltdown about the font on the place cards. It’s all very *rustic crisis*. Can’t wait to see you. And Leo…?*

I typed back. *On train. Have shoes. Tell mom the font is biodegradable. Leo is auditing the sandwich wrappers. See you tomorrow.*

*>> God. Well, try to make him smile. Or at least not lecture the officiant about the carbon cost of his words.*

I showed the phone to Leo. He read it, his expression not changing. “The officiant’s travel will be the primary carbon cost. His words are negligible.”

“That’s the spirit,” I said, dryly.

The train slowed, pulling into a small station. Swift Current. The name felt like a promise of movement, of clarity. Our stop. As we gathered our things, the reality of the next leg descended on me. Forty kilometers. On a bike. With a dress.

We wrestled the bikes onto the platform. The air here was different. It wasn’t cleaner, exactly—you could smell dust, diesel, the sweet-rotten scent of a nearby field—but it was *bigger*. It filled your lungs with space, not just molecules. Leo immediately checked his sensor.

“Wind’s from the northwest. Fifteen kilometers an hour. It’ll be a crosswind for the first leg, then a headwind when we turn south.” He said it like a meteorologist reporting a minor inconvenience on Mars.

I strapped my overstuffed panniers on, the bike groaning in protest. Leo’s were sleek, balanced, minimal. He looked at my setup, his lips tightening, but he said nothing. He just handed me a tube of sunscreen. “UV index is extreme. Reapply every hour.”

And then we were off, pedalling out of the sleepy town and onto a secondary highway, the pavement shimmering with heat. The first ten kilometers were a shock to my system. The crosswind shoved at us, a constant, invisible hand trying to push us into the ditch. My city legs burned. The vastness was overwhelming; the sky took up ninety percent of the world. There were no buildings to block the view of our own insignificance.

Leo, of course, was a machine. He found a rhythm and held it, his body a study in efficient motion. He didn’t speak, just pointed out hazards—a pothole, a piece of tire debris. His silence wasn’t hostile; it was focused. He was in his element, navigating a real-world problem with physical laws.

After an hour, we turned south onto a quieter grid road. And met the headwind.

It was like pedalling through syrup. A wall of air, hot and relentless, poured against us. Every revolution of the pedals was a fight. My breath came in ragged gasps. The romanticism of the journey evaporated, replaced by a base, animal struggle.

“We should have factored this more!” I shouted over the roar in my ears.

“I did!” he called back. “This is within the predicted range!”

“It feels personal!”

He didn’t answer. He just dropped back slightly, positioning his bike to break the wind for me. A drafting technique. He was sacrificing his own efficiency for mine. It was a simple, physical act of care, so unlike his usual clinical protections. It almost undid me.

We crawled along. The landscape changed, grew rougher. The flat fields gave way to rolling, sandy hills, studded with scrubby pine and poplar. The Cypress Hills, ancient, isolated uplands rising from the prairie. They looked bruised and beautiful, a fortress of green in a tawny sea.

My phone, mounted on my handlebars, dinged. A low battery warning. Then, no service. We were off the map.

The sun began its slow descent, painting the sky in violent oranges and purples. The temperature dropped. My sweat went cold. We still had maybe fifteen kilometers to the park gate, and then another five to the actual wedding site. In the dark. On bikes that now felt like anchors.

“Leo,” I said, my voice thin. “We’re not going to make it before dark.”

He checked his watch, a bulky solar-powered thing. “Probability of arrival before civil twilight is now less than ten percent.” He looked up, scanning the horizon. The road was empty. The only sound was the wind and our own laboured breathing. “We have a decision.”

I knew what he was thinking. The spreadsheet. The plan. The audit. Arriving after dark was a violation of the park’s rules, a blight on the perfect “low-impact” story. It would involve phone calls, rangers, explanations. It would be a failure.

I also thought of the card in my pannier. Twenty clicks away. Down a grid road.

“Silas,” I said, the word ripped away by the wind.

Leo’s jaw tightened. He’d been thinking it too. “It’s a deviation. Unplanned. It adds variables.”

“It adds a roof! A bed! It’s getting dark, Leo. And cold.”

He looked at me—really looked. My hair was a wild tangle, my face streaked with dust and sweat. I was shivering. The data point of my physical distress was in front of him. He weighed it against the purity of the plan.

“The carbon cost of a potential rescue, if we become hypothermic or have a mechanical failure, would far outweigh the cost of a detour,” he said, finally, as if convincing himself. “And the detour is closer. It is the logical choice.”

“Thank God for logic,” I muttered, already turning my bike towards the east, onto the smaller, rougher road his GPS indicated.

The last leg was a blur of pain. The road was gravel, chewed up by farm equipment. The wind was blocked now by hills, but the going was rough and slow. Dusk settled, a deep blue bleeding into black. The first stars appeared, sharp and cold. I’d never seen so many. In the city, you were lucky to see three. Here, the sky was a dusting of diamond grit. It was terrifying in its beauty.

And then, a light. A single, yellow square in the darkness, at the end of a long lane. The farmhouse.

We rolled into the yard, our bike lights cutting weak cones through the dark. The house was old, two-storey, white paint peeling. A porch wrapped around it. Beyond, the shapes of barns and silos loomed. A dog barked, a deep, echoing sound. The door opened, and Silas stood silhouetted against the warm light, holding a mug.

He didn’t look surprised. “Cut it a bit fine, didn’t you?” he called out.

We dismounted, our legs wobbly. I could barely speak. Leo, ever practical, managed, “The headwinds were thirty percent stronger than the historical average.”

Silas came down the porch steps. He was wearing worn jeans and a plaid shirt, open over a t-shirt. He looked at our bikes, our exhausted faces. “Historical averages aren’t what they used to be. Come in. You look done in.”

The house was nothing like our apartment. It was cluttered, warm, and smelled of woodsm



---

# Chapter 4

## **Chapter 4**

The first thing I ever stole from Silas was his quiet.

It wasn’t on purpose. It was the first morning. The air in the farmhouse tasted of woodsmoke and coffee and the deep, fungal damp of earth that no pavement could ever quite seal away. I woke on a couch that smelled of dog and old velvet, a quilt heavy as a confession over me. The roar of the fans was gone, replaced by a silence so thick it had texture—the creak of a settling beam, the scuttle of something in the walls, the distant, lowing complaint of a cow. It was a living silence, and it pressed against my city-sharpened edges, making them feel flimsy.

Leo was already up. I found him at the kitchen table, a chipped ceramic mug of water beside him, his tablet alive with scrolling data. He was mapping the previous day’s wind resistance against his predictive models, his brow furrowed in a kind of devotional concentration. Silas stood at the stove, cracking eggs into a cast-iron skillet. The sizzle was a small, domestic explosion.

“Sleep through the apocalypse, did you?” Silas said, not turning around. His voice was gravel wrapped in wool.

“I think we pedaled through it yesterday,” I said, my own voice raspy. Every muscle was a separate, singing ache.

“Fuel,” Silas said, sliding eggs onto two plates. “Eat. Then we work. You’re behind.”

“Behind what?” Leo asked, looking up, instantly alert.

“The weather,” Silas said, sitting with his own plate. He ate with a steady, methodical pace. “A system’s coming in three days. Not the gentle, regretful smoke of your city burns. A proper coastal bruiser. If you’re not through the mountain pass before it hits, you’ll be wintering here. And I don’t run a bed and breakfast.”

It was the first beat of a new rhythm. The farmhouse wasn’t a sanctuary; it was a pit stop. A recalibration. Silas, we learned, was a former civil engineer who’d “retired” to a life of paranoid self-sufficiency a decade ago. He was Leo’s great-uncle, a fact Leo mentioned only in passing, as if kinship were another data point of moderate relevance. The farm was a network of failing systems held together by ingenuity and spite. Our payment for roof and bed was labor.

**Scene 1: The team undergoes a series of trials that strengthen their bonds and skills.**

The trials were not epic. They were muddy, cold, and profoundly specific.

Silas had a way of teaching that was less instruction and more controlled exposure to failure. He pointed to the old diesel generator in the barn, a hulking, rusted thing. “It’s coughing. Fix it.”

Leo approached it like a surgeon, tablet in hand, searching for a manual. I approached it like a thing that owed me money. Silas watched from a stool, sipping coffee.

“The fuel-air mixture is likely suboptimal,” Leo began, peering at the engine. “We should calculate the compression ratio and…”

“It’s got water in the fuel, you idiot,” Silas said, not unkindly. “Drained water from the filter. See the separator? Clear bowl. Cloudy.”

I saw it. A sediment of water and fuel. Leo saw the theory made manifest. I learned to bleed the line, my hands turning black with grime and diesel. Leo learned the tactile click of a wrench fitting a bolt, the way force had a language. We got it running. The barn filled with the thunderous, sputtering roar of it, and Silas gave a single, slow nod. It felt like a graduation.

Next was the water pump from the well. Then patching a section of the poly tunnel where the plastic had frayed, fighting the wind that snatched at the tape and our hair. We hauled sacks of feed, our bodies protesting in unison. Leo calculated the most efficient load distribution and carrying formation. I told him to shut up and lift with his legs.

In the city, our partnership had been a silent contract of necessity. Here, it became a dialogue. I was all instinct and impatient leverage. He was all calculation and cautious procedure. We failed, separately. The generator wouldn’t start until I listened to his diagnosis of a fouled spark plug. The patch on the poly tunnel blew out until he followed my brute-force method of double-strapping it with cord.

One afternoon, Silas took us to the edge of his woodlot. The system was a day closer, and the sky was the color of a fresh bruise. “Heat,” he said. “The house eats it. You’re going to replenish the pile.”

He handed us a two-person crosscut saw, a thing of terrifying simplicity. “No chainsaw. Fuel’s for the generator. Your muscles are free.”

It was a brutal, beautiful dance. We faced each other over a fallen pine, our hands on the saw’s wooden handles. The first pulls were jerky, out of sync. The saw bucked and stuck in the green wood.
“You’re pulling against me,” I grunted.
“You’re pushing on the return stroke. It binds,” he countered.
We stopped, breathing plumes in the cold air. We looked at each other, not as city refugees, but as two parts of a single, inefficient machine. Silas watched, saying nothing.

“On three,” Leo said. “Pull. One, two, *three*.”

We pulled. The saw bit, slid back. We found a rhythm. Pull, relax. Pull, relax. The teeth sang through the wood, spitting out pale, fragrant chips. It was meditative. Exhausting. Our breath synced. The rhythm became instinct. When the log finally fell, the *thump* was a shared victory. We were slick with sweat despite the cold, our shoulders burning. We didn’t speak. We just looked at the clean cut, then at each other. A new data point, solid as timber: we could fit our edges together. We could make a useful tool.

**Scene 2: A betrayal or revelation redefines who an ally or enemy truly is.**

The revelation came that night, not with a shout, but with the soft click of a door.

We were in the cluttered living room, the woodstove throwing a desperate, hungry heat. Silas was showing Leo schematics of the mountain pass on his ancient, wheezing laptop—topography, old logging roads, the skeletal remains of a firewatch tower. I was ostensibly mending a tear in my gear, but mostly I was listening, soaking in the details like a dry sponge.

The talk turned to the city, to the reasons for our flight. Leo was clinical, describing the data-siphoning, the algorithmic pressures, the quiet, systematic closure of his research into atmospheric remediation. “They weren’t suppressing it,” he said, his voice flat. “They were weaponizing it. Tailoring it for private climate havens. My work was going to become a luxury good.”

Silas nodded, as if this was the most obvious outcome in the world. “And you?” he asked, his eyes shifting to me. They were the color of the flint we’d used to spark the stove. “What’d you steal that was worth this?”

It was the first time anyone had framed it that way. I’d been running *from*, not *with*. I set my mending down. “I didn’t steal anything. I just… left.”

“Everyone’s running from something,” Silas said. “Or to something. Usually, it’s the same thing.”

He got up, muttering about checking the livestock. The door to the kitchen swung shut behind him. Leo leaned closer to the laptop, zooming in on a section of the map. I felt a restlessness, a need for air that wasn’t thick with woodsmoke and unspoken history. I got up and walked to the kitchen for a glass of water.

Silas wasn’t outside. He was in the pantry, a small, cold room off the kitchen. The door was open a crack. And he was on the phone.

“...just arrived, like you said they would.” A pause. His voice was low, but the house was so quiet it carried. “No, she’s not what I expected. Tougher. The boy’s exactly as described. A calculator with legs.” Another pause. I froze, the glass in my hand turning to ice. “I’ll keep them here until the system moves in. It’ll box them in nicely. The pass will be impassable. They’ll have to sit tight until you can send the retrieval team. Yeah. I’ve got the coordinates.”

The world didn’t tilt. It sharpened. Every detail in the dim kitchen—the grain of the wooden counter, the chipped edge of the sink, the slow drip from the tap—became hyper-real. The warmth of the house curdled into a trap. Silas wasn’t a refuge. He was a gatekeeper. A shepherd, corralling the lambs for slaughter.

I backed away, silent, my heart a frantic drum against my ribs. I didn’t hear the rest. I didn’t need to. The word “retrieval team” was a key that turned the lock on everything. Leo’s people. Or the people who wanted what was in Leo’s head. It didn’t matter. Silas was the enemy.

I returned to the living room, my blood singing with a cold, clear fury. Leo looked up. “The elevation gain here is significant, but if we—”

“We need to leave,” I said, my voice barely a whisper. “Now.”

He blinked. “The system isn’t due for another two days. Silas said—”

“Silas is lying.” I told him what I’d heard, the words tumbling out in a harsh, urgent rush. I watched his face process it. The confusion, the denial, the slow, dawning horror as the logic of it assembled itself. The convenient detour. The warm welcome. The stalling repairs. It was all a script.

“But he’s family,” Leo said, the word sounding foreign in his mouth.

“He’s a data point,” I hissed. “And the data says he’s been compromised.”

The betrayal wasn’t passionate. It was transactional. Silas had sold us, or was being paid to hold us. Our bond, the trials, the shared rhythm of the saw—it was all just set-dressing for a cage. The revelation redefined the farmhouse. The warmth wasn’t shelter; it was the glow of the oven. The quiet wasn’t peace; it was the silence before the snare snaps shut.

**Scene 3: The protagonist learns a crucial new ability or gains a key piece of knowledge.**

We couldn’t run. Not yet. The bikes were in the barn. Silas was between us and the barn. The night was absolute, and we didn’t know the land. We were trapped, but now we knew the walls were there. That knowledge was a kind of power.

We pretended nothing was wrong. It was the hardest performance of my life. We drank the tea Silas made. We listened to his stories about the old logging roads. All the while, I was studying him not as a host, but as a obstacle. I watched how he moved—a slight favoring of his right knee. I noted where his eyes went when a sound came from outside—instantly to the window over the sink, which had the clearest view of the lane. I saw the shotgun, broken open and ostensibly unloaded, leaning by the back door. A prop, or a promise.

The crucial knowledge came later, in the deepest part of the night. I couldn’t sleep. The silence was now a listening thing. I heard Silas’s heavy tread on the stairs, the creak of his bedroom floor. Then, nothing.

A plan, half-formed and desperate, coiled in my mind. We needed a distraction. We needed to know the land. And I needed to not feel like a rat in a wall.

I slipped from the couch. In the kitchen, by the weak glow of the night-light over the stove, I saw it: Silas’s heavy waxed-canvas coat hanging by the door. On impulse, I slid my hands into the pockets. My fingers brushed against cold metal and paper. A small, old-fashioned compass. And a map, folded thick and soft with use.

I took them. The theft felt different this time. It wasn’t an accident of birth, taking Leo’s air. It wasn’t the desperate grab of food or gear. This was deliberate. This was a choice to take back my own trajectory. The compass was a small, heavy truth in my palm. The map was a lattice of possibilities.

Back in the living room, under the quilt with a flashlight, I opened it. It was a detailed topographic map of the county, marked with Silas’s tight, precise handwriting. *Spring—dry most years. *Old deer trail—washed out ’23. *Fenceline—electrified. And there, snaking away from the back of the property, through a stand of marked timber, was a thin, dashed line labeled *Tractor path. Seasonal. Connects to fire road 7.2.*

It wasn’t the main road. It wasn’t the way we’d come. It was a back door. A secret even the GPS wouldn’t know. The knowledge was a physical warmth in my chest. We weren’t at Silas’s mercy. We had a thread to follow out of the labyrinth.

I showed Leo in the morning, while Silas was out doing chores. We hunched over the map at the table, our heads close together. “This fire road,” Leo whispered, tracing it with a finger. “It adds twelve point four miles, and the grade is steeper, but it bypasses the main pass entirely. It links up with the old highway on the other side of the ridge.”

“Can we do it?” I asked.
He calculated. The weight of the bikes, our remaining food, the forecast he’d downloaded before we lost city signal. “Before the system hits? If we leave tonight. And if we don’t stop.”

The new ability wasn’t on the map. It was in me. The ability to see a person not as a character, but as a system with flaws—a bad knee, a predictable line of sight, a sentimental attachment to an old coat. The ability to hold a secret, to let it burn in my gut without a flicker in my eyes. The ability to navigate by betrayal.

**Scene 4: The path to the central ordeal becomes clear, but is fraught with greater danger.**

We spent the day as model prisoners. We helped Silas move hay bales in the barn. I made a point of admiring the shotgun. “Ever need to use it?” I asked, my voice casual.

“For coyotes,” he said, not looking at me. “And the occasional two-legged varmint who gets too curious.” He slid a glance my way. It felt like a physical touch.

We ate an early supper. The air had changed. It was heavier, charged. The wind had died completely, a sinister calm. Silas kept looking out the window at the bruised sky. “System’s speeding up,” he grunted. “Might be here by tomorrow night.”

He was lying. He wanted us to think we had more time. We knew we had none.

When he went upstairs for the night, we moved. We had packed our panniers in secret, taking only what was essential. We left the quilt folded neatly on the couch. We left a note, simple and damning in its politeness: *Thanks for everything. Didn’t want to overstay our welcome.*

The bikes were in the barn. The main door would squeal like a damned soul. We used the side door, a narrow, rotting thing that led into the equipment shed. We wheeled the bikes through the dark, our every sense screaming. The dog, chained by the porch, whined softly but didn’t bark. We were ghosts.

The tractor path was where the map said it would be, a faint depression in the tall, dead grass behind the last silo. It was not a road. It was a suggestion. It plunged immediately into the woods, a tunnel of blackness under a ceiling of groaning pines. We switched on our bike lights. The beams were pathetic, childish things against that swallowing dark, illuminating the ruts and rocks that wanted to buck us into the trees.

This was the path. Not the heroic mountain pass. This sneaking, back-alley route through the woods. It was clear, and it was ours. And it was fraught with a danger that had nothing to do with gradient or wind-chill.

We pedaled, not for speed, but for a desperate, forward momentum. The trail was brutal. Mud sucked at our tires. Branches, invisible until the last second, lashed our faces and caught on our gear. We pushed as much as we rode, our breath ragged in the absolute stillness. The world had shrunk to the three feet of light in front of our tires and the sound of our own straining bodies.

After an hour, Leo stopped, holding up a hand. We listened. Above the pound of blood in my ears, I heard it. Faint, far behind us, but unmistakable in the dead quiet: the high, angry whine of an ATV.

Silas knew.
He wasn’t waiting for a retrieval team. He was coming himself.

The path ahead was no longer just a physical challenge. It was a race. The greater danger wasn’t the storm at our backs, or the treacherous ground under our wheels. It was the man who knew this land, who knew where this path led, and who was now chasing us through the dark with the certain, grim purpose of a man who’d been paid to deliver a package.

We looked at each other, our faces ghastly in the LED glow. No words were needed. The bond forged by the crosscut saw, the trust tempered by betrayal—it all crystallized into this single, shared understanding.

We mounted our bikes and plunged deeper into the tunnel of trees, the beam of our lights a fragile lifeline into a darkness that now had a heartbeat, and it was gaining.



---

# Chapter 5

## **Chapter 5**

The first thing I ever stole from Leo was his oxygen. The first thing I ever stole from Mara was her certainty.

It wasn’t on purpose. It was the woods. The air here tasted of pine rot and cold, mineral earth, a scent so deep it was less a smell and more a pressure against the teeth. The controlled burn was far behind us now, replaced by a different kind of containment—the dense, watching stillness of old growth. We had pushed our bikes for another twenty minutes after hearing the ATV, until the trail became a narrow, root-gnarled seam along the shoulder of a ravine. The sound of the engine had faded, swallowed by the terrain or by Silas’s own tactical patience. It didn’t matter. The knowing was a third presence with us, a cold spot between our shoulder blades.

We stopped where the ravine flattened into a small, rocky clearing, a natural cul-de-sac. The wind, which had been a distant sigh, dropped to nothing. The silence was absolute, a vacuum.

“We can’t outrun him on the trail,” Leo said, his voice a low rasp that seemed to be absorbed by the moss before it reached me. He wasn’t looking at us. He was looking at his hands, turning them over as if reading a map in the scrapes and dirt. “He knows it. We know it. The trail is a funnel.”

Mara leaned her bike against a lichen-crusted boulder. Her movements were economical, precise, but there was a tremor in the line of her jaw, a tiny, persistent pulse. “So we don’t run on the trail.”

“The terrain off-trail is suicide,” I said. The words felt obvious, stupid. “Cliffs, deadfall, ravines you don’t see until you’re in them.”

“I’m not talking about terrain,” she said. She unzipped her pack and pulled out the worn topographical map, spreading it on a flat rock. In the beam of her headlamp, the concentric lines looked like a fingerprint. “I’m talking about expectation. He expects prey. Prey runs. Prey follows the path of least resistance.” Her finger stabbed a point just ahead of our estimated position. “Here. The ravine walls steepen for about a quarter mile. The trail is the only sane route through.”

Leo finally looked up. “And?”

“And we stop being sane.” Her finger traced a line that went directly up the eastern slope of the ravine, where the contour lines crowded so tightly they nearly merged. “We go up. We go over. We come down behind him.”

The audacity of it was a physical shock, a splash of cold water. It wasn’t a plan; it was a geometric rebellion. It treated the mountain not as an obstacle, but as a tool. I stared at her, at the fierce, untamed light in her eyes. This was the woman who had methodically packed a first-aid kit, who had calculated our caloric needs to the gram. Now she was proposing a vertical, off-map scramble in the dark to ambush our hunter.

“It’s not in the binders, Mara,” I said, and it came out softer than I intended, almost pleading.

A ghost of a smile touched her lips. “No. It’s not.”

Leo was studying the map, his brow furrowed. “The grade is… extreme. We’d have to leave the bikes. Carry everything. In the dark.”

“Yes.”
“And if we’re wrong about his position? If he’s ahead of us?”
“Then we’ve lost our bikes and wasted energy. And we die tired.” She said it with a chilling finality. “But the ATV sound came from behind. He’s following. He’s methodical. He’ll stick to the trail until he has reason not to.”

There it was. The pivot. Not a democratic discussion, not a weighing of options. It was a diagnosis and a prescription. Her certainty was a towering, impregnable thing. It had carried us this far, this fragile, city-born rebellion against a system that ground landscapes into data points. I had clung to it, this new gospel of preparedness and grit. Now, faced with its most radical commandment, I felt a deep, instinctive rift. This wasn’t a plan born of her binders. It was something older, wilder. It felt less like strategy and more like a sacrifice.

“You’re sure,” Leo said. It wasn’t a question.

Mara met his gaze. “I am the only one who is. That’s the point.”

The gender dynamics of our trio, until now, had been a subtle, unspoken chemistry. Leo, the physical anchor, his strength a quiet, reliable fact. Mara, the strategic mind, the architect of our defiance. Me, the chronicler, the thief of moments, trying to bridge the two. A neat, complementary triangle. Her pronouncement shattered that geometry. This wasn’t complementary. This was hierarchical. Her certainty wasn’t offering a choice; it was demanding a surrender. A surrender Leo, with a slow, grim nod, was prepared to give.

I saw the shift in his posture. The questioning tension left his shoulders. He was handing her the reins not just of the plan, but of the peril. It was a transaction I didn’t understand—a man ceding authority to a woman in the face of violent, physical danger. It wasn’t chivalry. It was colder, more pragmatic. He trusted her calculus more than he trusted his own instinct to run or fight. It was the bond of the crosscut saw, refined in the crucible of betrayal, now manifesting as absolute tactical submission.

It terrified me.

“We cache the bikes here,” Leo said, already moving. “Strip them of anything useful. Lights, tools.”

We worked in a frenetic, silent hurry. The bikes, our lifelines for so long, became mere objects, sources of parts. We hid them under a canopy of low, thick spruce boughs, brushing out our tracks. My Bianchi, a thing of beautiful, responsive mechanics, looked like a skeleton being buried. We redistributed the gear into our packs. The weight was obscene. It wasn’t just food and shelter. It was the weight of the choice.

Mara led the way to the ravine’s edge. Up close, the slope was a dark, sheer nightmare. It wasn’t a climb of rock, but of crumbling soil, exposed roots, and loose shale. It was the land’s rotten core.

“We go on all fours,” she said. “Three points of contact at all times. Test every handhold, every root. If you dislodge a rock, call it. Quietly.”

She didn’t wait for confirmation. She simply turned and began to climb.

***

The journey was not a journey. It was an excavation.

We clawed our way out of the ravine not as humans moving through a landscape, but as burrowing things trying to escape a grave. The world dissolved into a universe of touch and strain. The beam of my headlamp showed only a tiny, chaotic patch of dirt, a gnarled root, the tread of Mara’s boot six inches from my face. The air grew colder, thinner. The sound of our breathing, of grit sliding under our gloves, of packs scraping against the earth, was a private, brutal symphony.

Mara moved with a terrifying, spider-like certainty. She found purchase where I saw only slope. She trusted roots that looked like dead men’s fingers. She didn’t hesitate. Her progress was a continuous, flowing line. Leo and I were a series of grunts, pauses, and desperate scrambles. The physical disparity was a raw, humiliating fact. My city lungs, my café-counter muscles, screamed in protest against this primordial labor. Leo was stronger, but he was heavier, his bulk a liability on the crumbling face.

Halfway up, my handhold—a thick, promising root—pulled free with a soft, tearing sound. I slid backward, gravel skittering, my heart vaulting into my throat. A hand clamped around my forearm with a force that bruised. Leo. He had anchored himself against a stunted tree and stopped my slide with a single, brutal jerk.

“I’ve got you,” he grunted, the strain vibrating through his grip.

Above us, Mara had stopped. She looked down, her face in shadow behind her light. “You’re okay. Move. Don’t stop.”

There was no comfort in her words. They were an order. Leo released me once I’d found a new hold. My forearm throbbed. The kindness of his rescue was overshadowed by Mara’s cold assessment of the situation’s continuity. We were components in her plan. Damaged, but functional. Keep moving.

After an eternity of vertical crawling, the slope began to relent. We hauled ourselves over a final, rocky lip and collapsed onto a narrow, forested bench. We lay on our backs, gasping, the stars a dizzying, indifferent swirl through the black lace of branches. The silence here was different. We were above the ravine, in the realm of the wind. It murmured through the high pines, a lonely, ancient sound.

Mara was already sitting up, consulting her compass, aligning the map. She wasn’t breathing hard.

“We’re here,” she whispered, pointing. “The trail curves along the ravine bottom, about two hundred feet below us. We follow this bench north for half a mile. There’s a choke point where the ravine narrows. We get above it. We wait.”

Leo sat up, wiping his face with a sleeve. “And then what, Mara? We drop rocks on him?”

She looked at him, and for the first time, I saw a crack in the certainty. Not doubt of the plan, but something more profound. A hollow place. “We stop him,” she said, and the words had no texture, no detail. They were a void.

“Stop him,” I echoed. “You mean kill him.”

The word hung in the cold air, a black, ugly shape. We had talked of evasion, of escape. We had never given this eventuality a name.

“He is a paid instrument of the extraction,” Mara said, her voice flat. “He will not be reasoned with. He will not turn back. His purpose is to retrieve the data and eliminate the complication. That’s us.” She was reciting from a manual that didn’t exist. “If he reaches the relay point with the data, everything we’ve done—the fire, the run, all of it—is for nothing. The land loses. Silas wins.”

“So we become killers,” Leo said. It wasn’t an accusation. It was a verification, like he was checking the specs on a piece of equipment.

“We become the consequence,” Mara corrected, but the distinction was tissue-thin, and it tore as soon as she said it. She looked from Leo’s resigned face to my horrified one. The dynamic shifted again, seismically. The hierarchy of certainty now demanded an executioner. She was volunteering, but she needed our complicity. She needed us to hold the moral ground steady while she planted her flag in it.

Leo’s silence was a deep, accepting well. He would follow her lead, even into this. The bond of the saw, of shared labor and shared betrayal, had mutated into something that could encompass this. My silence was a scream trapped in ice. I had stolen oxygen, moments, trust. I had never bargained for this theft of innocence, for the weight of a life, even a life like Silas’s, being placed on the scales Mara had built.

“There has to be another way,” I said, the words feeble.

“Name it,” Mara said, and her eyes were not pleading. They were challenging. They were the eyes of the mountain itself, offering only raw, uncompromising facts. Wind, rock, gradient. Hunter, prey.

I had no name for another way. I had only stories, metaphors, regrets. Useless currencies here.

Leo stood, shouldering his pack. “Let’s get to the choke point.”

It was the vote that counted.

***

The final doubts did not surface as words. They were a climate.

We moved along the forest bench, a ghostly procession. The internal conflict was a silent rot spreading through us. In Leo, it manifested as a hyper-vigilant quiet, his eyes constantly scanning, his body coiled tighter than I’d ever seen it. He was preparing himself, muscle by muscle, synapse by synapse, for an act his soul had never blueprinted. He was building a door in a wall where no door had been, and the construction was tearing him apart from the inside.

In Mara, the doubt was a chilling efficiency. Every step was measured, every glance at the map was a ritual of reconfirmation. She was pouring every ounce of her being into the mechanics of the ambush—the placement, the timing, the angles—as if perfect execution could sanctify the act. She was not wrestling with the moral calculus; she was burning the ledger. The certainty had become a frantic, focused energy, a star burning itself out.

In me, it was a paralyzing clarity. I saw us not as heroes or rebels, but as three animals backed into a corner of evolution we were not equipped to handle. We were playing a game whose final move was murder, with rules written in a language only Mara seemed fluent in. The gender lens through which I’d seen our group—Leo’s strength, Mara’s mind—was now a distorting prism. This wasn’t about strength or strategy anymore. It was about the capacity to bear the unforgivable. And it was Mara, not Leo, who carried that capacity like a born talent. It was she who had led us up the impossible slope, and it was she who was now leading us to the lip of this moral ravine. Leo’s role was to follow, to lend his strength to her will. My role was to witness. The triangle was broken. We were a spear, and Mara was the point.

We reached the choke point. The ravine below pinched in, the trail forced between a sheer rock face and a precipitous drop. Above it, our bench culminated in a rocky overlook, a natural balcony strewn with football-sized stones.

This was the place.

Mara pointed to a massive, stable boulder. “Leo. You’re there. You have the best arm. When he’s directly below, you drop the rock. Aim for the ATV first. Disable it. Then him.”

Leo walked to the boulder. He didn’t touch the rocks. He just stared at them, his hands at his sides.

“What if we just block the trail?” I whispered, a last, desperate gambit. “A rockfall. Just trap him, don’t… don’t hit him.”

Mara shook her head, a short, sharp movement. “He has tools. He has time. He’d clear it or go around. It has to be decisive. It has to be him.”

The finality was absolute. She had moved beyond persuasion. She was in the territory of necessity, a country with brutal, simple laws.

We took our positions. Leo behind his boulder, a selection of stones arranged before him like grim artillery. Mara and I flattened ourselves behind a fallen log, a few feet back from the edge. We switched off our lights. The dark rushed in, complete and suffocating.

Now, there was only the wait. The wind. The cold seeping through our clothes. The sound of my own heartbeat, a frantic drum counting down to an unknown hour.

The fear was no longer of Silas. It was of the moment after. It was of looking into Leo’s eyes when the stone left his hand. It was of seeing what remained in Mara’s when it was done. It was of discovering what remained in my own.

We lay there for an hour. Two. The cold became part of our bones. The tension was a wire drawn taut between us, humming with unspoken dread.

Then, we heard it.

Not the whine of the ATV. A softer, more methodical sound. The crunch of boots on gravel. The occasional scrape of metal against rock.

He was on foot. He’d left the ATV behind, finding the ravine too narrow or too risky. He was proceeding silently, professionally.

The sound grew nearer. A slow, relentless cadence.

Mara nudged me, her eyes wide in the faint starlight. She pointed to Leo, then made a downward motion with her hand. *Wait.*

Leo’s silhouette behind the boulder was a statue of tension. He had a rock in his hands, hefting its weight.

The footsteps were directly below the overlook now. I could hear the soft rustle of his gear. I could almost smell the oil and cold sweat.

Mara raised her hand, ready to signal.

This was the entrance to the greatest danger. Not the physical trap below us. The moral one we were perched above. The point of no return was not a line on the map. It was a split-second of silent communication between Mara’s hand and Leo’s muscles.

I held my breath. The world narrowed to Mara’s raised fingers, silhouetted against the star-dusted sky.

Then, from the trail below, Silas’s voice cut through the dark. It wasn’t a shout. It was a calm, conversational tone, carrying perfectly in the cold air.

“I know you’re up there, city kids.”

We froze. The wire of tension snapped, replaced by a electric shock of exposure.

“I’ve known since the creek crossing two miles back. You leave a sign a blind man could follow.” A pause. The crunch of a single step closer. “You picked a good spot. For an ambush.”

My blood turned to ice. He wasn’t prey walking into a trap. He was a hunter who had followed the bait into a killing zone of his own choosing.

Mara’s hand was still raised, but it was trembling now. The certainty, her towering, impregnable certainty, shattered. It didn’t crumble; it exploded into a thousand glittering shards of dread. The plan, the climb, the moral sacrifice—it had all been seen, tracked, and allowed. We weren’t the architects of this moment. We were the specimens in his jar.

Leo looked over at us, his face a mask of stark, unvarnished terror. The rock in his hands was no longer a tool. It was a dead weight.

Silas’s voice came again, closer still, right at the choke point’s throat.

“Come on down. We can talk about the data. Or,” he said, and the casualness of it was more terrifying than any threat, “I can come up. And we can talk about what happens to people who try to drop rocks on me.”

He knew. He knew everything. The gender dynamics, the shifted leadership, the desperate, violent pivot—none of it mattered. We had climbed out of the ravine only to find him waiting for us on the higher ground.

Mara’s hand dropped. The signal would never come. The ambush was over before it began.

We were at the entrance to the greatest danger, and the door was already open, and Silas was standing in it, inviting us in.



---

# Chapter 6

## **Chapter 6**

The first thing I ever gave back to Leo was the chance to run.

It wasn’t a gift. It was a transaction, paid for in the currency of his terror and my own hollowing certainty. The roar of the industrial fans in my memory was nothing compared to the silence that followed Silas’s invitation. It was the silence of a vacuum, the kind that exists between stars—absolute, and lethal to anything that breathes. The white-noise scream was inside us now, in the static of our frozen blood.

Mara’s lowered hand didn’t fall to her side. It drifted down to press against the rough granite of the ledge, fingers splayed as if reading a brailled epitaph. Her plan, her beautiful, brutal architecture, lay in ruins around us, and he had been the one to supply the blueprints. We were city kids. We thought in terms of ambush points and choke holds, of leverage and decisive force. He thought in terms of terrain, and patience, and the inevitable mistakes of the panicked. We had climbed for an hour to gain this height. He had simply let us.

Leo made a sound. It wasn’t a word. It was the soft, choked exhale of a man realizing the ground beneath him is actually a ledge, and the ledge is giving way. The rock in his hands slipped, thudding dully against the stone by his boots. The sound was obscenely loud.

“Don’t do that,” Silas called up, his voice a study in mild reproach. “Might roll. Hit someone.” He was a shadow among shadows at the base of the choke point, a thicker patch of dark that occasionally caught a sliver of starlight on a boot toe or a shoulder seam. He hadn’t moved to climb. He was waiting. The specimen jar had a lid, and he was in no hurry to screw it tight.

Mara found her voice. It was cracked, stripped of its commanding timber. “What do you want?”

“The data,” Silas said, as if reminding a forgetful child. “The little drive you lifted from the Green Canopy’s back office. The one with the shipping manifests for the ‘sustainably sourced’ beans that come from the clear-cut zones.” He paused. “And a conversation.”

“We don’t have it here,” I said. The words felt like gravel in my mouth.

“I know,” Silas said. “It’s in the false bottom of your toolkit, back at your camp by the three pines. The one with the blue tent peg marking the north corner. Clever. But not clever enough.”

The ice in my veins became a single, solid glacier. He hadn’t just followed us. He had inventoried us. He had been inside our sanctuary while we were out building our trap. The gender dynamics of our group—Mara’s fierce strategy, Leo’s brute-force readiness, my own anxious logistics—were just curiosities to him. Behavioral notes. He had watched the mouse build its maze, and he knew where the cheese was.

Leo was shaking. It started in his hands, a fine tremor, then traveled up his arms to his shoulders. The terror was metabolizing, turning from paralysis into a wild, animal need for motion. Fight or flight, but the fight had been designed, choreographed, and now sat in the third row, laughing. Only flight remained.

“He’s one man,” Leo whispered, the words a ragged tear in the silence. He wasn’t talking to us. He was trying to convince the chemistry of his own body. “One man.”

“One man who knew you were here,” I whispered back, the logic a cold anchor. “Who knows where we sleep. Who didn’t come up shooting.” That was the most terrifying part. The casualness. The *conversational tone*. Violence was a tool in his belt, but he hadn’t reached for it. He was using something else: the absolute authority of being the one who sees everything.

Mara pushed herself upright. The movement was stiff, the shattering of her certainty physically painful to witness. She was putting herself back together, but the pieces were in the wrong order. The resolve was still there, but it was glazed with a new, desperate sheen. “If we give you the data?”

“Then we have a conversation about why you took it. About who you work for. About whether you’re useful idiots or malicious ones.” Silas’s shadow shifted. A boot scraped on stone. “If you don’t, I come up. We have a different conversation. It’s shorter. It involves the rock you just dropped, Leo, but not in your hands.”

The offer was no offer at all. It was a diagnosis. We were a problem to be solved, and he was presenting the two treatment plans: invasive questioning, or traumatic extraction.

Leo’s breathing hitched. The flight instinct won. It happened not with a shout, but with a whimper. He turned, his boots scrambling for purchase on the sloping ledge behind us—the one that led not down the choke point, but back along the cliff face, a narrower, more treacherous path we’d noted and dismissed as a retreat route.

“Leo, don’t—” Mara’s command was a ghost of itself.

He was already moving, his big body clumsy with fear, hugging the rock face. He didn’t get three steps.

The sound was a crisp *thwip*, not a gunshot. Something small and dark streaked from Silas’s position below. It wasn’t aimed at Leo. It struck the rock face a foot above his head with a sharp *crack*. A puff of stone dust bloomed in the starlight.

Leo froze, pinned.

“Painted marker round,” Silas said, his voice still calm. “Hurts like hell. Can blind you if you’re lucky. Rupture an eye if you’re not. The next one finds a softer target. Sit down, Leo.”

Leo didn’t sit. He slid down the rock, collapsing into a crouch, his arms over his head. A low, wounded noise escaped him. The sacrifice wasn’t of his body, not yet. It was of his spirit. The big man, the muscle, the rock-lifter, had been reduced to a cowering child by a sound and a puff of dust. The loss was immediate and profound. Our beloved ally wasn’t gone; he was dismantled before us.

And in that dismantling, something in me turned over.

It wasn’t courage. Courage is a fuel, hot and bright. This was colder. It was the recognition of a pattern, the same way I used to recognize the pattern of Leo’s breathing in the next bunk, or the specific whine of the third-floor fan bearing about to seize. Silas saw terrain. He saw patterns of behavior. He saw us as predictable, panicked animals. My whole life had been a study in the patterns of systems—the café’s supply chain, the city’s air filtration schedules, the way people move in crowds. He was just another system. A terrifyingly efficient one. But systems have inputs and outputs. They have pressure points.

He had tracked our panic. He had predicted our ambush. He had mapped our camp.

But he was alone.

And he was down there, and we were up here.

Mara was staring at Leo’s crumpled form, her face a mask of anguish and fury. She was the strategist, and her strategy was ash. She was the leader, and her follower was broken. The hierarchy we’d built, where her will was the keystone, was gone. The dynamic shifted, not with a struggle, but with a vacuum.

I spoke. My voice didn’t sound like my own. It was flat, drained of the anxious vibrato that usually lived in it. It was the voice of someone reading a meter. “You’re alone.”

A beat of silence from below. “Sufficient.”

“The Green Canopy is a front for the Northfall Consortium,” I said, the words coming out in a steady, uninflected stream. I was inputting data. “The manifests prove they’re sourcing from protected watersheds they’re supposedly replanting. We took it to expose them. We’re not working for anyone. We’re just in the way.”

“Acknowledged,” Silas said. “Now, come down.”

“You knew all that already,” I continued. “Or you guessed. You’re not security. They’d have sent five men and drones. You’re… clean-up. Independent contractor. Which means you have a choice. You can clean up the data. Or you can clean up us. The first is easier. But you followed us here instead of just raiding our camp. You want the conversation. You want to know if we’ve shared it. If there are copies. If the problem is contained.”

I could feel Mara’s gaze on me, sharp, shocked. Leo had gone still in his crouch.

Another pause, longer this time. The shadow at the base of the cliff shifted slightly. I had his attention. I had disrupted the pattern. He expected more panic, more pleading, more futile aggression. He wasn’t getting it.

“You’re the logistician,” Silas said finally. A note of… not respect. Interest. “The one who worries about the food packs and the water filters. Go on.”

He was engaging. The system was accepting input.

“You have a problem,” I said. “If you kill us, it’s a mess. Bodies are a mess. Even out here. You’d have to dispose. It’s inefficient. If you just take the data and scare us, we might talk. We might go to the authorities. We might be a persistent problem. Also inefficient.”

“Your point, logistician?”

“You need us to disappear willingly,” I said. The cold logic of it was a path unfolding in the dark. “You need us to be so terrified we bury this and never dig it up. You need our compliance, not our corpses. That’s why you’re talking. That’s why you’re showing us how easily you could hurt us. You’re not a hunter in a killing zone. You’re a teacher giving a lesson. The first lesson was that you see everything. The second lesson is that you decide everything.”

The silence that followed was different. The vacuum had a new quality: assessment.

“You’re smarter than your file suggested,” Silas said. It was the first real piece of information he’d given. He had a file. On us.

“My file was probably about my anxiety,” I said, a strange, detached laugh bubbling in my throat. “My caffeine dependency. My tendency to over-prepare. It wouldn’t mention that I see systems. And you, Silas, are a system that runs on control. Total, absolute control. But you don’t have it.”

“I have a marker rifle pointed at your friend.”

“You have one marker rifle. You have one angle. You are at the base of a narrow chute. To get the data, you need to either get us down, or go get it yourself. If you come up, you lose the high ground on one of us. Maybe two. It’s a risk. If we come down, we’re in your control. That’s your preferred outcome. So you’re applying pressure. But pressure can be applied from more than one direction.”

I moved. Not toward the edge. Not toward Leo. I took one step sideways, to the very lip of the ledge on the opposite side from the choke point. The side that looked out over the sheer drop into the main ravine, a two-hundred-foot fall into darkness and jagged rock.

“What are you doing?” Mara hissed, true fear in her voice for the first time.

I ignored her. I looked down into the abyss. The star-dusted sky was above, the void below. I raised my voice. “Your system requires a predictable response to threat. Cowering. Bargaining. Submission. But there’s another response. Erasure. You can’t control a decision to step into the void.”

Silas’s shadow went very still. “That’s not a solution. That’s a tantrum.”

“It’s a new variable,” I said. My heart was hammering against my ribs, a frantic bird in a cage, but my voice held its flat, metered calm. “Your file didn’t account for it. You don’t have a contingency for it. If I go over, Mara and Leo are still here. But the dynamic changes. The lesson fails. There’s a body, eventually found. There’s a story. There’s a *why*. You don’t want a *why*, Silas. You want a quiet lesson and a quiet disappearance. A *why* is a loose end. And you don’t do loose ends.”

I inched my boot forward. Pebbles skittered over the edge, vanishing without a sound.

“Stop,” Silas said. The casualness was gone. The tone was now a wire, taut and vibrating.

I had found the pressure point. Not in his body, but in his system. His need for clean, controlled outcomes.

“The data is just data,” I said. “You can destroy it. But you can’t destroy the fact that three people knew. The only way to make that fact not matter is to make us complicit in our own silence. You need our fear to be *productive*. My fear right now isn’t productive. It’s destructive. It’s a system error. And you have to fix it.”

I turned my head, just enough to look at Mara. Her eyes were wide, reflecting starlight. She was the strategist, but I was playing a game she didn’t know the rules to. I was betting our lives on the aesthetics of a clean job.

“Let them go,” I said, turning back to the darkness below. “Let Leo and Mara walk back to camp, get the data drive, and leave it on the stone at the creek crossing. They take nothing. They tell no one. They walk out of these mountains and forget any of this ever happened. You get your clean solution. The data is contained. The witnesses are compliant.”

“And you?” Silas asked. The wire in his voice was tightening.
